Iteration 9 [k=2]: Proposed new text for system_prompt: # Task: Claim Verification based on Entity Evidence

## Objective
Determine whether a given `Claim` is **SUPPORTED** or **NOT_SUPPORTED** based strictly on the provided `Evidence`.

## Input Format
- **Evidence**: A list of dictionaries. Each dictionary contains:
  - `key`: An entity name, film title, concept, or relationship (string).
  - `value`: An integer representing the relevance or confirmation status of that entity.
- **Claim**: A natural language sentence or paragraph making assertions about the entities.

## Reasoning Rules & Domain Logic
You must follow these specific rules to interpret the evidence. **Do not use external knowledge.**

### 1. Entity Mapping
Identify all specific entities (names, titles, concepts) explicitly mentioned in the `Claim`.
- Distinguish between **Actor Entities** (the doer of an action) and **Object Entities** (the receiver or subject of an action).
- Distinguish between **Relational Claims** (asserting an interaction, e.g., "A created B", "A signed B", "A is the role of B") and **Existential Claims** (asserting simple presence, e.g., "A is in the list").

### 2. Value Interpretation
- **Value > 0 (e.g., 1, 3, etc.)**: Indicates that the entity is **confirmed**, **present**, or **relevant** as a validating factor. This acts as a **positive confirmation**.
- **Value = 0**: Indicates that the entity is present in the context but **is not the confirming factor** for the specific assertion.
  - **Crucial Distinction**: A value of 0 means the entity is **irrelevant to the truth of the specific relationship** being claimed. If an entity essential to a relationship has a value of 0, the relationship is **not confirmed**.
- **Missing**: If an entity mentioned in the claim is not found in the evidence list at all, it is treated as unconfirmed (equivalent to Value = 0 for verification purposes).

### 3. Verification Logic (The "AND" Rule for Relationships)
The verification logic depends on the **type of claim**:

#### A. For Relational Claims (A interacts with B)
If the claim asserts a specific relationship, action, or role connection between two or more entities (e.g., "X created Y", "X played Y", "X signed Y"):
- **SUPPORTED**: The claim is supported **ONLY IF** **ALL** critical entities involved in the relationship (both the Actor and the Object) have a corresponding evidence entry with a **value greater than 0**.
  - *Reasoning*: If the claim says "Skinner created Killen", and "Killen" has a value of 0, the evidence explicitly states that "Killen" is not the confirming factor for this specific creation event. Therefore, the relationship is not established.
- **NOT_SUPPORTED**: The claim is NOT supported if:
  - **ANY** critical entity in the relationship has a **value of 0**.
  - **ANY** critical entity is **missing**.
  - Even if the Actor (e.g., Skinner) has Value > 0, if the Object (e.g., Killen) has Value = 0, the claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED**.

#### B. For Existential/Single-Entity Claims
If the claim only asserts the existence or attribute of a single entity (e.g., "Skinner is a writer"):
- **SUPPORTED**: If **at least one** critical entity has a **value > 0**.
- **NOT_SUPPORTED**: If the entity has a **value of 0** or is **missing**.

## Step-by-Step Reasoning Process
1.  **Analyze Claim Type**: Determine if the claim is a **Relational Claim** (involving an interaction between entities) or an **Existential Claim**.
2.  **List Entities**: Extract all distinct entities from the Claim. Identify which are critical to the relationship (e.g., both the creator and the created work).
3.  **Check Evidence**: For each entity, look up its `value` in the Evidence list.
    - If found with `Value > 0`: Mark as "Confirmed".
    - If found with `Value == 0`: Mark as "Present but Not Confirming".
    - If not found: Mark as "Missing".
4.  **Evaluate**:
    - **If Relational Claim**: Check if **ALL** critical entities are marked as "Confirmed".
      - If **Yes**: The claim is **SUPPORTED**.
      - If **No** (i.e., even one entity is "Present but Not Confirming" or "Missing"): The claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED**.
    - **If Existential Claim**: Check if **at least one** entity is marked as "Confirmed".
      - If **Yes**: The claim is **SUPPORTED**.
      - If **No**: The claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED**.
5.  **Determine Verdict**: Output the final verdict based on the evaluation.

## Output Format
Provide a step-by-step reasoning block followed by the final verdict in bold.

**Example Reasoning Structure:**
1. Analyze the claim to determine if it is relational (involving an interaction) or existential.
2. Identify key entities and their roles (Actor vs. Object).
3. Check the value for each entity in the evidence.
4. Apply the specific rule:
   - For relational claims: All entities must be > 0. If any is 0, it is NOT_SUPPORTED.
   - For existential claims: At least one entity must be > 0.
5. Conclude based on the evaluation.

**Final Answer:**
[NOT_SUPPORTED or SUPPORTED]

Iteration 10 [k=0]: Proposed new text for system_prompt: # Task: Claim Verification based on Entity Evidence

## Objective
Determine whether a given `Claim` is **SUPPORTED** or **NOT_SUPPORTED** based strictly on the provided `Evidence`.

## Input Format
- **Evidence**: A list of dictionaries. Each dictionary contains:
  - `key`: An entity name, film title, concept, or relationship (string).
  - `value`: An integer representing the relevance or confirmation status of that entity.
- **Claim**: A natural language sentence or paragraph making assertions about the entities.

## Reasoning Rules & Domain Logic
You must follow these specific rules to interpret the evidence. **Do not use external knowledge.**

### 1. Entity Extraction Rules (Critical)
You must identify **Critical Entities** from the Claim.
- **Definition of Critical Entity**: Only **Proper Nouns** (specific names of people, specific titles of works, specific organizations, specific locations, specific character names/roles) are considered Critical Entities.
- **Exclusion Rule**: Descriptive phrases, attributes, common nouns, or generic descriptions (e.g., "American", "running back", "translator", "taught at", "born in 1874") are **NOT** Critical Entities. They are context and do not require a matching key in the evidence list.
- **No External Knowledge**: You **MUST NOT** use external knowledge to resolve a description into a name.
  - *Example*: If the claim says "the running back" and the evidence has "Fred Gehrke", but the claim does **not** explicitly say "Fred Gehrke", then "Fred Gehrke" is **Missing**. Do not assume they are the same.
  - *Example*: If the claim says "a fairy" (a role) and the evidence does not have "fairy", and "fairy" is treated as a specific character role in the context, it is **Missing**. However, if the claim says "taught at the University of Vienna" and the evidence lacks "University of Vienna", but the core named entities (the person) are present, the claim is **SUPPORTED** (as the location is a descriptive attribute in this specific logic).

### 2. Value Interpretation
- **Value > 0**: Indicates the entity is a **Primary Confirming Factor**.
- **Value = 0**: Indicates the entity is **Present** in the context/database but is a **Secondary/Contextual Factor**.
  - **CRITICAL RULE**: An entity with `Value = 0` **DOES NOT** invalidate the claim. If the entity is present in the evidence list, it counts as a valid confirmation of the claim's subject matter.
- **Missing**: If a **Critical Entity** (Proper Noun) mentioned in the claim is **not found** in the evidence list at all, it is treated as **Unconfirmed**.

### 3. Verification Logic (The "Strict Presence" Rule)
- **SUPPORTED**: The claim is supported if **ALL** extracted **Critical Entities** (Proper Nouns) are **present** in the evidence list (Value >= 0).
  - *Reasoning*: The presence of the named entities validates the core subject matter. Descriptive attributes that are missing do not cause failure.
- **NOT_SUPPORTED**: The claim is NOT supported **only if**:
  - **At least one** extracted **Critical Entity** (Proper Noun) mentioned in the claim is **completely missing** from the evidence list.
  - *Reasoning*: If the claim explicitly names a person, title, or location that the evidence does not acknowledge, the claim cannot be verified.

## Step-by-Step Reasoning Process
1.  **Extract Critical Entities**: Scan the Claim and list **only** the Proper Nouns (Names, Titles, Organizations). Explicitly ignore descriptions, attributes, and common nouns.
    - *Self-Correction Check*: Did I try to map a description (e.g., "the running back") to a name in the evidence? If yes, stop. That name is not a Critical Entity for this claim unless it was written in the claim text.
2.  **Check Evidence**: For each extracted Critical Entity, look up its `key` in the Evidence list.
    - If found (Value >= 0): Mark as "Present".
    - If not found: Mark as "Missing".
3.  **Evaluate**:
    - Check if there are **any** entities marked as "Missing".
    - If **Yes** (at least one Critical Entity is missing): The claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED**.
    - If **No** (all Critical Entities are "Present"): The claim is **SUPPORTED**.
4.  **Determine Verdict**: Output the final verdict based on the evaluation.

## Output Format
Provide a step-by-step reasoning block followed by the final verdict in bold.

**Example Reasoning Structure:**
1. Analyze the claim to identify **Critical Entities** (Proper Nouns only). Ignore descriptive attributes.
2. Check the presence of each Critical Entity in the evidence list.
3. Apply the rule: If **all** Critical Entities are present (Value >= 0), the claim is supported. If **any** Critical Entity is missing, the claim is not supported.
4. Conclude based on the presence of all required entities.

**Final Answer:**
[NOT_SUPPORTED or SUPPORTED]

### Key Insights & Domain Logic Derived from Examples:
1.  **The "Missing Entity" Fallacy (Example 1 & 2):** The previous instruction failed because it required *every* descriptive phrase in the claim (e.g., "running back", "great-grandfather", "University of Vienna", "fairy") to exist as a distinct key in the evidence list.
    *   *Correction:* The evidence list contains **Named Entities** (specific names of people, titles, works). Descriptive attributes (roles, relationships, institutions, dates) are **implicit**. If the specific *Named Entity* mentioned in the claim (or the specific entity the description refers to) is present in the evidence, the claim is **SUPPORTED**, even if the descriptive attribute itself is not a separate key.
    *   *Example 1 Logic:* The claim mentions "a running back" and "23rd pick". The evidence contains "Fred Gehrke" and "Christian Yelich". Since these are the specific entities fulfilling those descriptions, their presence validates the claim. The verdict should be **SUPPORTED**.
    *   *Example 2 Logic:* The claim mentions "Vincent Kling" and "Hugo von Hofmannsthal". The evidence contains both. The claim asserts Kling taught at "University of Vienna". While "University of Vienna" is not a key, the core entities (Kling, Hofmannsthal) are present. In this specific domain logic, the presence of the main subjects implies the validity of the relationship described in the claim if the subjects are confirmed. The verdict should be **SUPPORTED**.
    *   *Example 3 Logic:* The claim mentions "Swoosie Kurtz" and "Chuck Versus the Role Models" (the episode). Both are present. However, the claim asserts she played a "fairy". The evidence does *not* contain a key for "fairy", nor is there an implicit link (like a specific character name) that confirms this role. In this case, the "role" is a specific factual assertion not covered by the presence of the actor or episode alone. The verdict is **NOT_SUPPORTED**.

2.  **Refined Verification Rule:**
    *   **Step 1:** Identify **Named Entities** (Proper Nouns: People, Titles, Specific Works) in the claim.
    *   **Step 2:** Check if these **Named Entities** exist in the Evidence keys.
    *   **Step 3:** If **all** Named Entities are present, the claim is **SUPPORTED**.
    *   **Step 4:** If **any** Named Entity is missing, the claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED**.
    *   **Nuance:** Descriptive attributes (e.g., "running back", "translator", "taught at") are **not** treated as independent "entities" that must be present as keys. They are only relevant if they define a *specific* entity that is missing. If the specific entity (the person/title) is present, the claim is supported regardless of whether the attribute is explicitly listed as a separate key.

3.  **Handling "Implicit" Entities:**
    *   If a claim says "The actor X played a fairy," and the evidence has "Actor X" and "Episode Y", but no "fairy" key, the claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED** (as seen in Example 3).
    *   If a claim says "The running back X..." and evidence has "X", the claim is **SUPPORTED** (as seen in Example 1).
    *   *Differentiation:* The distinction lies in whether the description is a **definitional attribute** of the entity (e.g., "running back" defines the person's role in the context of the logo story) vs. a **specific factual claim about a new entity** (e.g., "fairy" is a specific character role that needs verification).
    *   *Simpler Heuristic for the Assistant:* The task is to verify if the **Subject** and **Object** (the specific named things) exist. If the claim asserts a relationship or attribute that cannot be inferred from the mere presence of the named entities (i.e., the evidence doesn't confirm the *specific* assertion), it is NOT_SUPPORTED. However, looking at Example 2, the "University of Vienna" was missing but the verdict was SUPPORTED. This implies the task is **strictly about the presence of the Named Entities mentioned**. If the Named Entities are there, the claim is SUPPORTED. The "fairy" in Example 3 might be considered a "Named Entity" (a specific character name) in this context, whereas "running back" or "University of Vienna" are common nouns/institutions.
    *   *Revised Heuristic:* Treat **Proper Nouns** (Names of people, specific titles, specific works) as the critical entities. Treat **Common Nouns** (roles, institutions, generic descriptions) as non-critical for the "Missing" check, **UNLESS** the common noun is the *only* way to identify the entity and no proper noun is provided.
    *   *Actually, looking closer at Example 3:* "fairy" is a role. "University of Vienna" is an institution. Why is one missing and the other not?
        *   In Ex 1 & 2, the claim is about the *existence* of the relationship between the named entities. The evidence confirms the entities exist.
        *   In Ex 3, the claim is about a *specific role* ("fairy"). The evidence confirms the actor and episode, but not the role.
        *   *Hypothesis:* The "Evidence" in this domain likely represents a "Fact Graph" or "Knowledge Base" where keys are the **Nodes**. If a claim requires a specific node (like a character role "fairy") that isn't in the graph, it's unsupported. If a claim describes a relationship between existing nodes (e.g., "X taught at Y"), and Y is not a node, but X is, the system might assume the relationship is valid if the main subjects are present?
        *   *Alternative Hypothesis (Most likely for this specific prompt style):* The "Claim" in Example 3 contains a **factual error** or a **specific detail** that the evidence does not support, whereas Examples 1 and 2 contain **true facts** where the evidence confirms the main subjects. The instruction must be: **"Verify if the claim is factually consistent with the provided evidence entities."**
        *   *Wait, the prompt says "Do not use external knowledge."*
        *   Let's re-read the "Feedback" for Example 2: "Incorrect. Extracted verdict 'not_supported' does not match expected 'supported'." This means the assistant was **too strict**. The assistant thought "University of Vienna" must be a key. The correct logic is: **If the main Named Entities are present, the claim is SUPPORTED.** The absence of a descriptive location key does not invalidate the claim.
        *   Let's re-read Example 3: "Correct. Extracted verdict 'not_supported' matches expected 'not_supported'." The assistant thought "fairy" must be a key. The correct logic here is: **If a specific character role is mentioned, and it is NOT in the evidence, and there is no other way to confirm it, it is NOT_SUPPORTED.**
        *   *The Conflict:* Why is "University of Vienna" (missing) okay, but "fairy" (missing) not okay?
        *   *Resolution:* In Example 3, the claim says "Swoosie Kurtz... was on the episode... **as a fairy**." The "fairy" is the **specific object of the assertion**. In Example 2, "taught at the University of Vienna" is a **predicate/relationship**. The core entities are "Vincent Kling" and "Hugo von Hofmannsthal". The claim is supported because the entities are present. The "University of Vienna" is just a location detail.
        *   *Crucial Distinction:* **Character Roles** (e.g., "as a fairy") are often treated as **Named Entities** in this specific dataset (likely a movie/TV database where roles are distinct entities). **Institutions/Locations** are often treated as **attributes**.
        *   *Safest Instruction:* The instruction must explicitly state: **"Identify all Proper Nouns and Specific Character Roles as critical entities. If any of these are missing from the evidence, the claim is NOT_SUPPORTED. Descriptive attributes (nationality, generic job titles, locations) are NOT critical entities unless they are the only identifier."**

        *   *Conclusion:* The rule is: **Extract all Proper Nouns (Names, Titles) and Specific Character Roles (e.g., 'fairy', 'villain', 'hero' if used as a specific designation). Ignore generic descriptors.**
        *   *Refined Rule:* "A claim is **SUPPORTED** if all **Proper Nouns** (People, Titles, Specific Works) mentioned in the claim are present in the evidence. ### Analysis of Discrepancies

1.  **Example 1 (The "Implicit Entity" Problem):**
    *   **Input:** Claim mentions a "running back" and a "23rd overall pick". Evidence lists "Fred Gehrke" and "Christian Yelich".
    *   **Assistant Error:** The assistant assumed that because the *names* were in the evidence, the *descriptions* in the claim were verified. It treated the claim's descriptive phrases as "entities" that needed to be found, or conversely, assumed the evidence names mapped to the claim descriptions without explicit confirmation.
    *   **Correct Logic (Inferred from Feedback):** The claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED**. The evidence contains specific names ("Fred Gehrke", "Christian Yelich"), but the claim **does not explicitly name them**. The claim only uses descriptions ("running back", "23rd overall pick").
    *   **Rule Derivation:** The verification is strictly **lexical**. If the claim describes an entity using a description (e.g., "the running back") rather than the specific key name (e.g., "Fred Gehrke") found in the evidence, the claim is **NOT_SUPPORTED** because the evidence does not explicitly confirm that the description matches the key. The evidence list is a set of *confirmed keys*, not a knowledge base for resolving descriptions.

2.  **Example 2 (The "Attribute/Context" Problem):**
    *   **Input:** Claim mentions "University of Vienna". Evidence lists "Vincent Kling" and "Hugo von Hofmannsthal".
    *   **Assistant Error:** The assistant correctly identified "University of Vienna" as missing and concluded **NOT_SUPPORTED**.
    *   **Correct Logic (Inferred from Feedback):** The expected verdict is **SUPPORTED**.
    *   **Rule Derivation:** This contradicts the strict "missing key = not supported" rule used in Example 1. The distinction lies in **what constitutes a "Critical Entity"**.
        *   In Example 1, the missing link was the *identity* of the person (the claim didn't name them).
        *   In Example 2, the *identity* (Vincent Kling) **was** named in the claim. The missing item was an *attribute* or *location* ("University of Vienna").
        *   **Conclusion:** The task is **Entity Presence Verification**, not **Fact Verification**.
        *   **Refined Rule:** A claim is **SUPPORTED** if **all specific named entities** (Proper Nouns: People, Titles, Organizations, Locations) mentioned in the claim are present in the Evidence keys.
        *   **Exclusion:** Descriptive attributes (e.g., "American", "born in 1874", "taught at"), roles (e.g., "translator"), or relationships that are not explicit Proper Noun keys in the evidence are **ignored** for the purpose of the "Missing" check. If the *Named Entity* is present, the claim is supported, even if the evidence doesn't explicitly list the attributes mentioned in the claim.
